# Mars

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Jump to navigation Jump to search

Fourth planet from the Sun in the Solar System

This article is about the planet. For the deity, see [Mars
(mythology)](/wiki/Mars_\(mythology\) "Mars \(mythology\)"). For other uses,
see [Mars (disambiguation)](/wiki/Mars_\(disambiguation\) "Mars
\(disambiguation\)").

**Mars** is the fourth [planet](/wiki/Planet "Planet") from the
[Sun](/wiki/Sun "Sun") and the second-smallest planet in the [Solar
System](/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"), being larger than only
[Mercury](/wiki/Mercury_\(planet\) "Mercury \(planet\)"). In English, Mars
carries the name of the [Roman god of war](/wiki/Mars_\(mythology\) "Mars
\(mythology\)") and is often referred to as the " **Red Planet** ".[16][17]
The latter refers to the effect of the [iron oxide](/wiki/Iron\(III\)_oxide
"Iron\(III\) oxide") prevalent on Mars's surface, which gives it a [reddish
appearance](/wiki/Mars_surface_color "Mars surface color") distinctive among
the astronomical bodies visible to the naked eye.[18] Mars is a [terrestrial
planet](/wiki/Terrestrial_planet "Terrestrial planet") with a thin
[atmosphere](/wiki/Atmosphere "Atmosphere"), with surface features reminiscent
of the [impact craters](/wiki/Impact_crater "Impact crater") of the
[Moon](/wiki/Moon "Moon") and the valleys, deserts and [polar ice
caps](/wiki/Polar_ice_caps "Polar ice caps") of [Earth](/wiki/Earth "Earth").

[The days and seasons](/wiki/Timekeeping_on_Mars "Timekeeping on Mars") are
comparable to those of Earth, because the [rotational
period](/wiki/Rotational_period "Rotational period") as well as the tilt of
the [rotational axis](/wiki/Rotational_axis "Rotational axis") relative to the
[ecliptic plane](/wiki/Ecliptic_plane "Ecliptic plane") are similar. Mars is
the site of [Olympus Mons](/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons"), the largest
[volcano](/wiki/Volcano "Volcano") and [highest known
mountain](/wiki/List_of_tallest_mountains_in_the_Solar_System "List of tallest
mountains in the Solar System") on any planet in the Solar System, and of
[Valles Marineris](/wiki/Valles_Marineris "Valles Marineris"), one of the
largest canyons in the Solar System. The smooth [Borealis
basin](/wiki/Borealis_basin "Borealis basin") in the Northern Hemisphere
covers 40% of the planet and may be a giant impact feature.[19][20] Mars has
[two moons](/wiki/Moons_of_Mars "Moons of Mars"),
[Phobos](/wiki/Phobos_\(moon\) "Phobos \(moon\)") and
[Deimos](/wiki/Deimos_\(moon\) "Deimos \(moon\)"), which are small and
irregularly shaped. These may be captured [asteroids](/wiki/Asteroid
"Asteroid"), similar to [5261 Eureka](/wiki/5261_Eureka "5261 Eureka"), a
[Mars trojan](/wiki/Mars_trojan "Mars trojan").[21][22]

[Mars has been explored](/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars "Exploration of Mars") by
several uncrewed spacecraft. _[Mariner 4](/wiki/Mariner_4 "Mariner 4")_ was
the first spacecraft to visit Mars; launched by [NASA](/wiki/NASA "NASA") on
28 November 1964, it made its closest approach to the planet on 15 July 1965.
_Mariner 4_ detected the weak Martian radiation belt, measured at about 0.1%
that of Earth, and captured the first images of another planet from deep
space.[23] The [Soviet](/wiki/Soviet_space_program "Soviet space program")
_[Mars 3](/wiki/Mars_3 "Mars 3")_ mission included a
[lander](/wiki/Lander_\(spacecraft\) "Lander \(spacecraft\)"), which achieved
a [soft landing](/wiki/Soft_landing_\(aeronautics\) "Soft landing
\(aeronautics\)") in December 1971; however, contact was lost seconds after
touchdown.[24] On 20 July 1976, _[Viking 1](/wiki/Viking_1 "Viking 1")_
performed the first successful landing on the Martian surface.[25] On 4 July
1997, the _[Mars Pathfinder](/wiki/Mars_Pathfinder "Mars Pathfinder")_
spacecraft landed on Mars and on 5 July released its
[rover](/wiki/Rover_\(space_exploration\) "Rover \(space exploration\)"),
_[Sojourner](/wiki/Sojourner_\(rover\) "Sojourner \(rover\)")_ , the first
robotic rover to operate on Mars.[26] The _[Mars Express](/wiki/Mars_Express
"Mars Express")_ orbiter, the first [European Space
Agency](/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency") (ESA) spacecraft
to visit Mars, arrived in orbit on 25 December 2003.[27] In January 2004,
NASA's [Mars Exploration Rovers](/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover "Mars
Exploration Rover"), named _[Spirit](/wiki/Spirit_\(rover\) "Spirit
\(rover\)")_ and _[Opportunity](/wiki/Opportunity_\(rover\) "Opportunity
\(rover\)")_ , both landed on Mars; _Spirit_ operated until 22 March 2010 and
_Opportunity_ lasted until 10 June 2018.[28] NASA landed its
_[Curiosity](/wiki/Curiosity_\(rover\) "Curiosity \(rover\)")_ rover on August
6, 2012, as a part of its [Mars Science Laboratory
(MSL)](/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory "Mars Science Laboratory") mission to
investigate Martian climate and geology.[29] On 24 September 2014, the [Indian
Space Research Organisation](/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation "Indian
Space Research Organisation") (ISRO) became the fourth space agency to visit
Mars when its maiden interplanetary mission, the [Mars Orbiter
Mission](/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission "Mars Orbiter Mission") spacecraft,
arrived in orbit.[30] The [United Arab Emirates](/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates
"United Arab Emirates") became the fifth to successfully undertake a mission
to Mars, having inserted an orbiter in to the Martian atmosphere on 9 February
2021.[31] NASA's [_Perseverance_](/wiki/Perseverance_\(rover\) "Perseverance
\(rover\)") rover and [_Ingenuity_](/wiki/Ingenuity_\(helicopter\) "Ingenuity
\(helicopter\)") helicopter successfully landed on Mars on 18 February
2021.[32] _Ingenuity_ successfully completed the first powered controlled
flight by an aircraft on any planet besides Earth on 19 April 2021, taking off
[vertically](/wiki/VTOL "VTOL"), hovering and landing on Mars. [33][34]

There are investigations assessing the past
[habitability](/wiki/Planetary_habitability "Planetary habitability") of Mars,
as well as the [possibility of extant life](/wiki/Life_on_Mars "Life on
Mars"). Astrobiology missions are planned, such as the European Space Agency's
_[Rosalind Franklin](/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin_\(rover\) "Rosalind Franklin
\(rover\)")_ rover.[35][36][37][38] Liquid [water on the surface of
Mars](/wiki/Water_on_Mars "Water on Mars") cannot exist due to low atmospheric
pressure, which is less than 1% of the atmospheric pressure on Earth, except
at the lowest elevations for short periods.[39][40][41] The two polar ice caps
appear to be made largely of water.[42][43] The volume of water ice in the
south polar ice cap, if melted, would be sufficient to cover the planetary
surface to a depth of 11 metres (36 ft).[44] In November 2016, NASA reported
finding a large amount of underground ice in the [Utopia
Planitia](/wiki/Utopia_Planitia "Utopia Planitia") region. The volume of water
detected has been estimated to be equivalent to the volume of water in [Lake
Superior](/wiki/Lake_Superior "Lake Superior").[45][46][47]

Mars can easily be seen from Earth with the naked eye, as can its reddish
coloring. Its [apparent magnitude](/wiki/Apparent_magnitude "Apparent
magnitude") reaches −2.94, which is surpassed only by [Venus](/wiki/Venus
"Venus"), [the Moon](/wiki/Moon "Moon") and [the Sun](/wiki/Sun "Sun").[12]
Optical ground-based telescopes are typically limited to resolving features
about 300 kilometres (190 mi) across when Earth and Mars are closest because
of Earth's atmosphere.[48]

## Nomenclature

In English, the planet is named for the Roman god of war,[49] an association
made because of its red color, which suggests blood.[50] The adjectival form
of Latin _Mars_ is _Martius_ ,[51] which provides the English words _Martian_
, used as an adjective or for a putative inhabitant of Mars, and _Martial_ ,
used as an adjective corresponding to _Terrestrial_ for Earth.[52] In Greek,
the planet is known as Ἄρης _Arēs_ , with the inflectional stem Ἄρε-
_Are-_.[53] From this come technical terms such as _[areology](/wiki/Areology
"Areology")_ , as well as the adjective _Arean_[54] and the star name
_[Antares](/wiki/Antares "Antares")_.

'Mars' is also the basis of the name of the month of
_[March](/wiki/March_\(month\) "March \(month\)")_ (from Latin _Martius
mēnsis_ 'month of Mars'),[55] as well as of _Tuesday_ (Latin _dies Martis_
'day of Mars'), where the old Anglo-Saxon god [Tíw](/wiki/T%C3%ADw "Tíw") was
identified with Roman god Mars by [Interpretatio
germanica](/wiki/Interpretatio_germanica "Interpretatio germanica").[56]

Due to the global influence of [European languages](/wiki/Indo-
European_language_family "Indo-European language family") in
[astronomy](/wiki/Astronomy "Astronomy"), a word like _Mars_ or _Marte_ for
the planet is common around the world, though it may be used alongside older,
native words. A number of other languages have provided words with
international usage. For example:

  * [Arabic](/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language") مريخ _mirrīkh_ – which connotes fire – is used as the (or a) name for the planet in [Persian](/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language"), [Urdu](/wiki/Urdu "Urdu"), [Malay](/wiki/Malay_language "Malay language") and [Swahili](/wiki/Swahili_language "Swahili language"),[57] among others
  * [Chinese](/wiki/Chinese_language "Chinese language") 火星 [Mandarin _Huǒxīng_ ] 'fire star' (in Chinese the five classical planets are identified with the [five elements](/wiki/Wuxing_\(Chinese_philosophy\) "Wuxing \(Chinese philosophy\)")) is used in [Korean](/wiki/Korean_language "Korean language"), [Japanese](/wiki/Japanese_language "Japanese language") and [Vietnamese](/wiki/Vietnamese_language "Vietnamese language").[58]
  * India uses the [Sanskrit](/wiki/Sanskrit_language "Sanskrit language") term _[Mangal](/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission "Mars Orbiter Mission")_ derived from the Hindu goddess [Mangala](/wiki/Mangala "Mangala").[59]
  * A long-standing nickname for Mars is the "Red Planet". That is also the planet's name in [Hebrew](/wiki/Hebrew "Hebrew"), מאדים _ma'adim_ , which is derived from אדום _adom_ , meaning 'red'.[60]
  * The archaic Latin form _Māvors_ ([/ˈmeɪvɔːrz/](/wiki/Help:IPA/English "Help:IPA/English")) is seen, but only very rarely, in English, though the adjectives _Mavortial_ and _Mavortian_ mean 'martial' in the military rather than planetary sense.[61]

## Physical characteristics

Mars is approximately half the diameter of Earth, with a surface area only
slightly less than the total area of Earth's dry land.[10] Mars is less dense
than Earth, having about 15% of Earth's volume and 11% of Earth's
[mass](/wiki/Mass "Mass"), resulting in about 38% of Earth's surface gravity.
The red-orange appearance of the Martian surface is caused by [iron(III)
oxide](/wiki/Iron\(III\)_oxide "Iron\(III\) oxide"), or rust.[62] It can look
like butterscotch;[63] other common surface colors include golden, brown, tan,
and greenish, depending on the [minerals](/wiki/Mineral "Mineral")
present.[63]

### Internal structure

Like Earth, Mars has [differentiated](/wiki/Planetary_differentiation
"Planetary differentiation") into a dense metallic [core](/wiki/Planetary_core
"Planetary core") overlaid by less dense materials.[64] Scientists initially
determined that the core is at least partially liquid.[65] Current models of
its interior imply a core with a radius of about 1,794 ± 65 kilometres (1,115
± 40 mi), consisting primarily of [iron and
nickel](/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93nickel_alloy "Iron–nickel alloy") with about 16–17%
[sulfur](/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur").[66] This [iron(II)
sulfide](/wiki/Iron\(II\)_sulfide "Iron\(II\) sulfide") core is thought to be
twice as rich in lighter elements as Earth's.[67] The core is surrounded by a
silicate [mantle](/wiki/Mantle_\(geology\) "Mantle \(geology\)") that formed
many of the [tectonic](/wiki/Tectonics_of_Mars "Tectonics of Mars") and
volcanic features on the planet, but it appears to be dormant. Besides silicon
and oxygen, the most abundant elements in the Martian
[crust](/wiki/Crust_\(geology\) "Crust \(geology\)") are [iron](/wiki/Iron
"Iron"), [magnesium](/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium"), [aluminium](/wiki/Aluminium
"Aluminium"), [calcium](/wiki/Calcium "Calcium"), and
[potassium](/wiki/Potassium "Potassium"). The average thickness of the
planet's crust is about 50 kilometres (31 mi), with a maximum thickness of 125
kilometres (78 mi).[67] Earth's crust averages 40 kilometres (25 mi).

Mars is seismically active, with [InSight](/wiki/InSight "InSight") recording
over 450 marsquakes and related events in 2019.[68][69] In March 2021, NASA
reported, based on measurements of over 500 [Marsquakes](/wiki/Marsquake
"Marsquake") by the [_InSight_ lander](/wiki/InSight "InSight") on the planet
Mars, that the core of Mars is between 1,810 and 1,860 km (1,120 and 1,160
mi), about half the size of the [core](/wiki/Earth%27s_outer_core "Earth's
outer core") of [Earth](/wiki/Earth "Earth"), and significantly smaller --
suggesting a core of lighter [elements](/wiki/Chemical_element "Chemical
element") -- than thought earlier.[70]

### Surface geology

Main article: [Geology of Mars](/wiki/Geology_of_Mars "Geology of Mars")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Mars-
topo-200-fast.gif)](/wiki/File:Mars-topo-200-fast.gif)

[](/wiki/File:Mars-topo-200-fast.gif "Enlarge")

The topographic map of Mars

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Mars-
albedo-200-fast.gif)](/wiki/File:Mars-albedo-200-fast.gif)

[](/wiki/File:Mars-albedo-200-fast.gif "Enlarge")

The albedo map of Mars

Mars is a [terrestrial planet](/wiki/Terrestrial_planet "Terrestrial planet")
whose surface consists of minerals containing [silicon](/wiki/Silicon
"Silicon") and [oxygen](/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), [metals](/wiki/Metal "Metal"),
and other elements that typically make up [rock](/wiki/Rock_\(geology\) "Rock
\(geology\)"). The Martian surface is primarily composed of
[tholeiitic](/wiki/Tholeiitic_magma_series "Tholeiitic magma series")
[basalt](/wiki/Basalt "Basalt"),[71] although parts are more
[silica](/wiki/Silica "Silica")-rich than typical basalt and may be similar to
[andesitic](/wiki/Andesitic "Andesitic") rocks on Earth, or silica glass.
Regions of low [albedo](/wiki/Albedo "Albedo") suggest concentrations of
[plagioclase feldspar](/wiki/Plagioclase_feldspar "Plagioclase feldspar"),
with northern low albedo regions displaying higher than normal concentrations
of sheet silicates and high-silicon glass. Parts of the southern highlands
include detectable amounts of high-calcium [pyroxenes](/wiki/Pyroxenes
"Pyroxenes"). Localized concentrations of [hematite](/wiki/Hematite
"Hematite") and [olivine](/wiki/Olivine "Olivine") have been found.[72] Much
of the surface is deeply covered by finely grained [iron(III)
oxide](/wiki/Iron\(III\)_oxide "Iron\(III\) oxide") dust.[73][74]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/USGS-MarsMap-
sim3292-20140714-crop.png/220px-USGS-MarsMap-
sim3292-20140714-crop.png)](/wiki/File:USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png)

[](/wiki/File:USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png "Enlarge")

[Geologic map](/wiki/Geology_of_Mars "Geology of Mars") of Mars
([USGS](/wiki/USGS "USGS"), 2014)[75]

Although Mars has no evidence of a structured global [magnetic
field](/wiki/Magnetic_field "Magnetic field"),[76] observations show that
parts of the planet's crust have been magnetized, suggesting that alternating
polarity reversals of its dipole field have occurred in the past. This
[paleomagnetism](/wiki/Paleomagnetism "Paleomagnetism") of magnetically
susceptible minerals is similar to the [alternating bands found on Earth's
ocean floors](/wiki/Plate_tectonics "Plate tectonics"). One theory, published
in 1999 and re-examined in October 2005 (with the help of the _[Mars Global
Surveyor](/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor "Mars Global Surveyor")_ ), is that these
bands suggest [plate tectonic activity](/wiki/Plate_tectonics "Plate
tectonics") on Mars four [billion](/wiki/1000000000_\(number\) "1000000000
\(number\)") years ago, before the planetary [dynamo](/wiki/Dynamo_theory
"Dynamo theory") ceased to function and the planet's magnetic field faded.[77]

It is thought that, during the [Solar System's
formation](/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System "Formation and
evolution of the Solar System"), Mars was created as the result of a
[stochastic process](/wiki/Stochastic_process "Stochastic process") of run-
away accretion of material from the [protoplanetary
disk](/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk "Protoplanetary disk") that orbited the Sun.
Mars has many distinctive chemical features caused by its position in the
Solar System. Elements with comparatively low boiling points, such as
[chlorine](/wiki/Chlorine "Chlorine"), [phosphorus](/wiki/Phosphorus
"Phosphorus"), and [sulphur](/wiki/Sulphur "Sulphur"), are much more common on
Mars than Earth; these elements were probably pushed outward by the young
Sun's energetic [solar wind](/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind").[78]

After the formation of the planets, all were subjected to the so-called "[Late
Heavy Bombardment](/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment")".
About 60% of the surface of Mars shows a record of impacts from that
era,[79][80][81] whereas much of the remaining surface is probably underlain
by immense impact basins caused by those events. There is evidence of an
enormous impact basin in the Northern Hemisphere of Mars, spanning 10,600 by
8,500 kilometres (6,600 by 5,300 mi), or roughly four times the size of the
Moon's [South Pole – Aitken basin](/wiki/South_Pole_%E2%80%93_Aitken_basin
"South Pole – Aitken basin"), the largest impact basin yet discovered.[19][20]
This theory suggests that Mars was struck by a [Pluto](/wiki/Pluto
"Pluto")-sized body about four billion years ago. The event, thought to be the
cause of the [Martian hemispheric
dichotomy](/wiki/Martian_hemispheric_dichotomy "Martian hemispheric
dichotomy"), created the smooth [Borealis basin](/wiki/Borealis_basin
"Borealis basin") that covers 40% of the planet.[82][83]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/An_artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_Mars_four_billion_years_ago.jpg/220px-
An_artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_Mars_four_billion_years_ago.jpg)](/wiki/File:An_artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_Mars_four_billion_years_ago.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:An_artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_Mars_four_billion_years_ago.jpg
"Enlarge")

Artist's impression of how Mars may have looked four billion years ago[84]

The geological history of Mars can be split into many periods, but the
following are the three primary periods:[85][86]

  * **[Noachian](/wiki/Noachian "Noachian") period** (named after [Noachis Terra](/wiki/Noachis_Terra "Noachis Terra")): Formation of the oldest extant surfaces of Mars, 4.5 to 3.5 billion years ago. Noachian age surfaces are scarred by many large impact craters. The [Tharsis](/wiki/Tharsis "Tharsis") bulge, a volcanic upland, is thought to have formed during this period, with extensive flooding by liquid water late in the period.
  * **[Hesperian](/wiki/Hesperian "Hesperian") period** (named after [Hesperia Planum](/wiki/Hesperia_Planum "Hesperia Planum")): 3.5 to between 3.3 and 2.9 billion years ago. The Hesperian period is marked by the formation of extensive lava plains.
  * **[Amazonian period](/wiki/Amazonian_\(Mars\) "Amazonian \(Mars\)")** (named after [Amazonis Planitia](/wiki/Amazonis_Planitia "Amazonis Planitia")): between 3.3 and 2.9 billion years ago to the present. Amazonian regions have few [meteorite impact](/wiki/Impact_event "Impact event") craters but are otherwise quite varied. [Olympus Mons](/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons") formed during this period, with lava flows elsewhere on Mars.

Geological activity is still taking place on Mars. The [Athabasca
Valles](/wiki/Athabasca_Valles "Athabasca Valles") is home to sheet-like lava
flows created about 200 [Mya](/wiki/Mya_\(unit\) "Mya \(unit\)"). Water flows
in the [grabens](/wiki/Graben "Graben") called the [Cerberus
Fossae](/wiki/Cerberus_Fossae "Cerberus Fossae") occurred less than 20 Mya,
indicating equally recent volcanic intrusions.[87] On 19 February 2008, images
from the _[Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter](/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter
"Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter")_ showed evidence of an avalanche from a
700-metre-high (2,300 ft) cliff.[88]

### Soil

Main article: [Martian soil](/wiki/Martian_soil "Martian soil")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg/220px-
Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg)](/wiki/File:Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg "Enlarge")

Exposure of silica-rich dust uncovered by the _[Spirit](/wiki/Spirit_\(rover\)
"Spirit \(rover\)")_ rover

The _[Phoenix](/wiki/Phoenix_\(spacecraft\) "Phoenix \(spacecraft\)")_ lander
returned data showing Martian soil to be slightly alkaline and containing
elements such as [magnesium](/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium"),
[sodium](/wiki/Sodium "Sodium"), [potassium](/wiki/Potassium "Potassium") and
[chlorine](/wiki/Chlorine "Chlorine"). These nutrients are found in soils on
Earth, and they are necessary for growth of plants.[89] Experiments performed
by the lander showed that the Martian soil has a
[basic](/wiki/Base_\(chemistry\) "Base \(chemistry\)") [pH](/wiki/PH "PH") of
7.7, and contains 0.6% of the [salt](/wiki/Salt_\(chemistry\) "Salt
\(chemistry\)") [perchlorate](/wiki/Perchlorate
"Perchlorate").[90][91][92][93] This is a very high concentration and makes
the Martian soil toxic (see also [Martian soil
toxicity](/wiki/Martian_soil#Toxicity "Martian soil")).[94][95]

[Streaks](/wiki/Dark_slope_streak "Dark slope streak") are common across Mars
and new ones appear frequently on steep slopes of craters, troughs, and
valleys. The streaks are dark at first and get lighter with age. The streaks
can start in a tiny area, then spread out for hundreds of metres. They have
been seen to follow the edges of boulders and other obstacles in their path.
The commonly accepted theories include that they are dark underlying layers of
soil revealed after avalanches of bright dust or [dust
devils](/wiki/Dust_devil "Dust devil").[96] Several other explanations have
been put forward, including those that involve water or even the growth of
organisms.[97][98]

### Hydrology

Main article: [Water on Mars](/wiki/Water_on_Mars "Water on Mars")

Liquid water cannot exist on the surface of Mars due to low atmospheric
pressure, which is less than 1% that of Earth's,[39] except at the lowest
elevations for short periods.[40][41] The two polar ice caps appear to be made
largely of water.[42][43] The volume of water ice in the south polar ice cap,
if melted, would be sufficient to cover the entire planetary surface to a
depth of 11 metres (36 ft).[44] A [permafrost](/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost")
mantle stretches from the pole to latitudes of about 60°.[42] [Large
quantities of ice](/wiki/Water_on_terrestrial_planets_of_the_Solar_System
"Water on terrestrial planets of the Solar System") are thought to be trapped
within the thick [cryosphere](/wiki/Cryosphere "Cryosphere") of Mars. Radar
data from _[Mars Express](/wiki/Mars_Express "Mars Express")_ and the _[Mars
Reconnaissance Orbiter](/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter "Mars Reconnaissance
Orbiter")_ (MRO) show large quantities of ice at both poles (July
2005)[99][100] and at middle latitudes (November 2008).[101] The Phoenix
lander directly sampled water ice in shallow Martian soil on 31 July
2008.[102]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg/220px-
Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg)](/wiki/File:Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg "Enlarge")

Photomicrograph by _[Opportunity](/wiki/Opportunity_\(rover\) "Opportunity
\(rover\)")_ showing a gray [hematite](/wiki/Hematite "Hematite")
[concretion](/wiki/Concretion "Concretion"), nicknamed "blueberries",
indicative of the past existence of liquid water.

[Landforms](/wiki/Geomorphology "Geomorphology") visible on Mars strongly
suggest that liquid water has existed on the planet's surface. Huge linear
swathes of scoured ground, known as [outflow channels](/wiki/Outflow_channels
"Outflow channels"), cut across the surface in about 25 places. These are
thought to be a record of erosion caused by the catastrophic release of water
from subsurface aquifers, though some of these structures have been
hypothesized to result from the action of glaciers or lava.[103][104] One of
the larger examples, [Ma'adim Vallis](/wiki/Ma%27adim_Vallis "Ma'adim Vallis")
is 700 kilometres (430 mi) long, much greater than the Grand Canyon, with a
width of 20 kilometres (12 mi) and a depth of 2 kilometres (1.2 mi) in places.
It is thought to have been carved by flowing water early in Mars's
history.[105] The youngest of these channels are thought to have formed as
recently as only a few million years ago.[106] Elsewhere, particularly on the
oldest areas of the Martian surface, finer-scale, dendritic [networks of
valleys](/wiki/Valley_networks_\(Mars\) "Valley networks \(Mars\)") are spread
across significant proportions of the landscape. Features of these valleys and
their distribution strongly imply that they were carved by
[runoff](/wiki/Surface_runoff "Surface runoff") resulting from precipitation
in early Mars history. Subsurface water flow and [groundwater
sapping](/wiki/Groundwater_sapping "Groundwater sapping") may play important
subsidiary roles in some networks, but precipitation was probably the root
cause of the incision in almost all cases.[107]

Along crater and canyon walls, there are thousands of features that appear
similar to terrestrial [gullies](/wiki/Gullies "Gullies"). The gullies tend to
be in the highlands of the Southern Hemisphere and to face the Equator; all
are poleward of 30° latitude. A number of authors have suggested that their
formation process involves liquid water, probably from melting ice,[108][109]
although others have argued for formation mechanisms involving carbon dioxide
frost or the movement of dry dust.[110][111] No partially degraded gullies
have formed by weathering and no superimposed impact craters have been
observed, indicating that these are young features, possibly still
active.[109] Other geological features, such as [deltas](/wiki/River_delta
"River delta") and [alluvial fans](/wiki/Alluvial_fans "Alluvial fans")
preserved in craters, are further evidence for warmer, wetter conditions at an
interval or intervals in earlier Mars history.[112] Such conditions
necessarily require the widespread presence of [crater
lakes](/wiki/Volcanic_crater_lake "Volcanic crater lake") across a large
proportion of the surface, for which there is independent mineralogical,
sedimentological and geomorphological evidence.[113]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Mars_exposed_subsurface_ice.jpg/220px-
Mars_exposed_subsurface_ice.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mars_exposed_subsurface_ice.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:Mars_exposed_subsurface_ice.jpg "Enlarge")

A cross-section of underground water ice is exposed at the steep slope that
appears bright blue in this enhanced-color view from the
[MRO](/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter "Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter").[114]
The scene is about 500 meters wide. The scarp drops about 128 meters from the
level ground. The ice sheets extend from just below the surface to a depth of
100 meters or more.[115]

Further evidence that liquid water once existed on the surface of Mars comes
from the detection of specific minerals such as [hematite](/wiki/Hematite
"Hematite") and [goethite](/wiki/Goethite "Goethite"), both of which sometimes
form in the presence of water.[116] In 2004, _Opportunity_ detected the
mineral [jarosite](/wiki/Jarosite "Jarosite"). This forms only in the presence
of acidic water, which demonstrates that water once existed on Mars.[117] More
recent evidence for liquid water comes from the finding of the mineral
[gypsum](/wiki/Gypsum "Gypsum") on the surface by NASA's Mars rover
Opportunity in December 2011.[118][119] It is estimated that the amount of
water in the upper mantle of Mars, represented by [hydroxyl
ions](/wiki/Hydroxyl_ion "Hydroxyl ion") contained within the minerals of
Mars's geology, is equal to or greater than that of Earth at 50–300 parts per
million of water, which is enough to cover the entire planet to a depth of
200–1,000 metres (660–3,280 ft).[120]

In 2005, radar data revealed the presence of large quantities of water ice at
the poles[99] and at mid-latitudes.[101][121] The Mars rover
_[Spirit](/wiki/Spirit_rover "Spirit rover")_ sampled chemical compounds
containing water molecules in March 2007.

On 18 March 2013, [NASA](/wiki/NASA "NASA") reported evidence from instruments
on the [_Curiosity_ rover](/wiki/Curiosity_\(rover\) "Curiosity \(rover\)") of
[mineral hydration](/wiki/Mineral_hydration "Mineral hydration"), likely
hydrated [calcium sulfate](/wiki/Calcium_sulfate "Calcium sulfate"), in
several [rock samples](/wiki/Rock_\(geology\) "Rock \(geology\)") including
the broken fragments of ["Tintina" rock](/wiki/Tintina_\(rock\) "Tintina
\(rock\)") and ["Sutton Inlier" rock](/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity
"List of rocks on Mars") as well as in [veins](/wiki/Vein_\(geology\) "Vein
\(geology\)") and [nodules](/wiki/Nodule_\(geology\) "Nodule \(geology\)") in
other rocks like ["Knorr" rock](/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity "List of
rocks on Mars") and ["Wernicke" rock](/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity
"List of rocks on Mars").[122][123][124] Analysis using the rover's [DAN
instrument](/wiki/Curiosity_\(rover\)#Dynamic_Albedo_of_Neutrons_\(DAN\)
"Curiosity \(rover\)") provided evidence of subsurface water, amounting to as
much as 4% water content, down to a depth of 60 centimetres (24 in), during
the rover's traverse from the _[Bradbury Landing](/wiki/Bradbury_Landing
"Bradbury Landing")_ site to the _Yellowknife Bay_ area in the
_[Glenelg](/wiki/Glenelg,_Mars "Glenelg, Mars")_ terrain.[122] In September
2015, NASA announced that they had found conclusive evidence of hydrated
[brine](/wiki/Brine "Brine") flows on [recurring slope
lineae](/wiki/Recurring_slope_lineae "Recurring slope lineae"), based on
spectrometer readings of the darkened areas of slopes.[125][126][127] These
observations provided confirmation of earlier hypotheses based on timing of
formation and their rate of growth, that these dark streaks resulted from
water flowing in the very shallow subsurface.[128] The streaks contain
hydrated salts, perchlorates, which have water molecules in their crystal
structure.[129] The streaks flow downhill in Martian summer, when the
temperature is above −23° Celsius, and freeze at lower temperatures.[130]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Perspective_view_of_Korolev_crater.jpg/290px-
Perspective_view_of_Korolev_crater.jpg)](/wiki/File:Perspective_view_of_Korolev_crater.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:Perspective_view_of_Korolev_crater.jpg "Enlarge")

Perspective view of Korolev crater shows 1.9 kilometres (1.2 mi) deep [water
ice](/wiki/Ice "Ice"). Image taken by [ESA](/wiki/European_Space_Agency
"European Space Agency")'s _[Mars Express](/wiki/Mars_Express "Mars
Express")_.

Researchers suspect that much of the low northern plains of the planet were
[covered with an ocean](/wiki/Mars_ocean_hypothesis "Mars ocean hypothesis")
hundreds of meters deep, though this remains controversial.[131] In March
2015, scientists stated that such an ocean might have been the size of Earth's
[Arctic Ocean](/wiki/Arctic_Ocean "Arctic Ocean"). This finding was derived
from the ratio of water to [deuterium](/wiki/Deuterium "Deuterium") in the
modern Martian atmosphere compared to that ratio on Earth. The amount of
Martian deuterium is eight times the amount that exists on Earth, suggesting
that ancient Mars had significantly higher levels of water. Results from the
_Curiosity_ rover had previously found a high ratio of deuterium in [Gale
Crater](/wiki/Gale_Crater "Gale Crater"), though not significantly high enough
to suggest the former presence of an ocean. Other scientists caution that
these results have not been confirmed, and point out that Martian climate
models have not yet shown that the planet was warm enough in the past to
support bodies of liquid water.[132]

Near the northern polar cap is the 81.4 kilometres (50.6 mi) wide [Korolev
Crater](/wiki/Korolev_\(Martian_crater\) "Korolev \(Martian crater\)"), where
the [Mars Express](/wiki/Mars_Express "Mars Express") orbiter found it to be
filled with approximately 2,200 cubic kilometres (530 cu mi) of water
ice.[133] The crater floor lies about 2 kilometres (1.2 mi) below the rim, and
is covered by a 1.8 kilometres (1.1 mi) deep central mound of permanent water
ice, up to 60 kilometres (37 mi) in diameter.[133][134]

In February 2020, it was found that dark streaks called recurring slope lineae
(RSL), which appear seasonably, are caused by briny water flowing for a few
days annually.[135][136]

#### Polar caps

Main article: [Martian polar ice caps](/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps "Martian
polar ice caps")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg/165px-
Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg)](/wiki/File:Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg)

North polar early summer water ice cap (1999); a seasonal layer of carbon
dioxide ice forms in winter and disappears in summer.

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg/219px-
South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg)](/wiki/File:South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg)

South polar midsummer ice cap (2000); the south cap has a permanent carbon
dioxide ice cap mixed with water ice.[137]

Mars has two permanent polar ice caps. During a pole's winter, it lies in
continuous darkness, chilling the surface and causing the
[deposition](/wiki/Deposition_\(phase_transition\) "Deposition \(phase
transition\)") of 25–30% of the atmosphere into slabs of
[CO2](/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") ice ([dry ice](/wiki/Dry_ice "Dry
ice")).[138] When the poles are again exposed to sunlight, the frozen CO2
[sublimes](/wiki/Sublimation_\(physics\) "Sublimation \(physics\)"). These
seasonal actions transport large amounts of dust and water vapor, giving rise
to Earth-like frost and large [cirrus clouds](/wiki/Cirrus_cloud "Cirrus
cloud"). Clouds of water-ice were photographed by the
_[Opportunity](/wiki/Opportunity_rover "Opportunity rover")_ rover in
2004.[139]

The caps at both poles consist primarily (70%) of water ice. Frozen carbon
dioxide accumulates as a comparatively thin layer about one metre thick on the
north cap in the northern winter only, whereas the south cap has a permanent
dry ice cover about eight metres thick. This permanent dry ice cover at the
south pole is peppered by [flat floored, shallow, roughly circular
pits](/wiki/Swiss_cheese_features "Swiss cheese features"), which repeat
imaging shows are expanding by meters per year; this suggests that the
permanent CO2 cover over the south pole water ice is degrading over time.[140]
The northern polar cap has a diameter of about 1,000 kilometres (620 mi)
during the northern Mars summer,[141] and contains about 1.6 million cubic
kilometres (5.7×1016 cu ft) of ice, which, if spread evenly on the cap, would
be 2 kilometres (1.2 mi) thick.[142] (This compares to a volume of 2.85
million cubic kilometres (1.01×1017 cu ft) for the [Greenland ice
sheet](/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet "Greenland ice sheet").) The southern polar
cap has a diameter of 350 kilometres (220 mi) and a thickness of 3 kilometres
(1.9 mi).[143] The total volume of ice in the south polar cap plus the
adjacent layered deposits has been estimated at 1.6 million cubic km.[144]
Both polar caps show spiral troughs, which recent analysis of
[SHARAD](/wiki/SHARAD "SHARAD") ice penetrating radar has shown are a result
of [katabatic winds](/wiki/Katabatic_wind "Katabatic wind") that spiral due to
the [Coriolis Effect](/wiki/Coriolis_Effect "Coriolis Effect").[145][146]

The seasonal frosting of areas near the southern ice cap results in the
formation of transparent 1-metre-thick slabs of dry ice above the ground. With
the arrival of spring, sunlight warms the subsurface and pressure from
subliming CO2 builds up under a slab, elevating and ultimately rupturing it.
This leads to [geyser-like eruptions](/wiki/Martian_geyser "Martian geyser")
of CO2 gas mixed with dark basaltic sand or dust. This process is rapid,
observed happening in the space of a few days, weeks or months, a rate of
change rather unusual in geology – especially for Mars. The gas rushing
underneath a slab to the site of a geyser carves a spiderweb-like pattern of
radial channels under the ice, the process being the inverted equivalent of an
erosion network formed by water draining through a single
plughole.[147][148][149][150]

### Geography and naming of surface features

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg/220px-
Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mars_topography_\(MOLA_dataset\)_with_poles_HiRes.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:Mars_topography_\(MOLA_dataset\)_with_poles_HiRes.jpg "Enlarge")

A [MOLA](/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Laser_Altimeter "Mars Orbiter Laser
Altimeter")-based topographic map showing highlands (red and orange)
dominating the Southern Hemisphere of Mars, lowlands (blue) the northern.
Volcanic plateaus delimit regions of the northern plains, whereas the
highlands are punctuated by several large impact basins.

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg/220px-
PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg)](/wiki/File:PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg "Enlarge")

These new impact craters on Mars occurred sometime between 2008 and 2014, as
detected from orbit

Main article: [Geography of Mars](/wiki/Geography_of_Mars "Geography of Mars")

Further information: [Areoid](/wiki/Areoid "Areoid")

See also: [Category:Surface features of
Mars](/wiki/Category:Surface_features_of_Mars "Category:Surface features of
Mars")

Although better remembered for mapping the Moon, [Johann Heinrich
Mädler](/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_M%C3%A4dler "Johann Heinrich Mädler") and
[Wilhelm Beer](/wiki/Wilhelm_Beer "Wilhelm Beer") were the first areographers.
They began by establishing that most of Mars's surface features were permanent
and by more precisely determining the planet's rotation period. In 1840,
Mädler combined ten years of observations and drew the first map of Mars.
Rather than giving names to the various markings, Beer and Mädler simply
designated them with letters; Meridian Bay (Sinus Meridiani) was thus feature
" _a_ ".[151]

Today, features on Mars are named from a variety of sources. [Albedo
features](/wiki/Classical_albedo_features_on_Mars "Classical albedo features
on Mars") are named for classical mythology. Craters larger than 60 km are
named for deceased scientists and writers and others who have contributed to
the study of Mars. Craters smaller than 60 km are named for towns and villages
of the world with populations of less than 100,000. Large valleys are named
for the word "Mars" or "star" in various languages; small valleys are named
for rivers.[152]

Large [albedo](/wiki/Albedo "Albedo") features retain many of the older names
but are often updated to reflect new knowledge of the nature of the features.
For example, _[Nix Olympica](/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons")_ (the snows of
Olympus) has become _[Olympus Mons](/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons")_ (Mount
Olympus).[153] The surface of Mars as seen from Earth is divided into two
kinds of areas, with differing albedo. The paler plains covered with dust and
sand rich in reddish iron oxides were once thought of as Martian "continents"
and given names like [Arabia Terra](/wiki/Arabia_Terra "Arabia Terra") ( _land
of Arabia_ ) or [Amazonis Planitia](/wiki/Amazonis_Planitia "Amazonis
Planitia") ( _Amazonian plain_ ). The dark features were thought to be seas,
hence their names [Mare Erythraeum](/wiki/Mare_Erythraeum "Mare Erythraeum"),
Mare Sirenum and [Aurorae Sinus](/wiki/Aurorae_Sinus "Aurorae Sinus"). The
largest dark feature seen from Earth is [Syrtis Major
Planum](/wiki/Syrtis_Major_Planum "Syrtis Major Planum").[154] The permanent
northern polar ice cap is named [Planum Boreum](/wiki/Planum_Boreum "Planum
Boreum"), whereas the southern cap is called [Planum
Australe](/wiki/Planum_Australe "Planum Australe").

Mars's equator is defined by its rotation, but the location of its [Prime
Meridian](/wiki/Prime_Meridian "Prime Meridian") was specified, as was Earth's
(at [Greenwich](/wiki/Greenwich "Greenwich")), by choice of an arbitrary
point; Mädler and Beer selected a line for their first maps of Mars in 1830.
After the spacecraft [Mariner 9](/wiki/Mariner_9 "Mariner 9") provided
extensive imagery of Mars in 1972, a small crater (later called
[Airy-0](/wiki/Airy-0 "Airy-0")), located in the [Sinus
Meridiani](/wiki/Sinus_Meridiani "Sinus Meridiani") ("Middle Bay" or "Meridian
Bay"), was chosen by [Merton Davies](/wiki/Merton_Davies "Merton Davies") of
the [Rand Corporation](/wiki/Rand_Corporation "Rand Corporation")[155] for the
definition of 0.0° longitude to coincide with the original selection.[156]

Because Mars has no oceans and hence no "[sea level](/wiki/Sea_level "Sea
level")", a zero-elevation surface had to be selected as a reference level;
this is called the _[areoid](/wiki/Areoid "Areoid")_[157] of Mars, analogous
to the terrestrial [geoid](/wiki/Geoid "Geoid").[158] Zero altitude was
defined by the height at which there is 610.5 [Pa](/wiki/Pascal_\(unit\)
"Pascal \(unit\)") (6.105 [mbar](/wiki/Bar_\(unit\) "Bar \(unit\)")) of
atmospheric pressure.[159] This pressure corresponds to the [triple
point](/wiki/Triple_point "Triple point") of water, and it is about 0.6% of
the sea level surface pressure on Earth (0.006 atm).[160]

#### Map of quadrangles

Main article: [List of quadrangles on Mars](/wiki/List_of_quadrangles_on_Mars
"List of quadrangles on Mars")

For mapping purposes, the [United States Geological
Survey](/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey "United States Geological
Survey") divides the surface of Mars into thirty cartographic
[quadrangles](/wiki/Quadrangle_\(geography\) "Quadrangle \(geography\)"), each
named for a classical albedo feature it contains. The quadrangles can be seen
and explored via the interactive image map below.

#### Impact topography

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/PIA23304-Mars-
ImpactCrater-Sep2016-Feb2019.jpg/220px-PIA23304-Mars-ImpactCrater-
Sep2016-Feb2019.jpg)](/wiki/File:PIA23304-Mars-ImpactCrater-
Sep2016-Feb2019.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:PIA23304-Mars-ImpactCrater-Sep2016-Feb2019.jpg "Enlarge")

Newly formed impact crater (est 2016 – 2019). False blue color highlights
exposed bedrock

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg/220px-
PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg)](/wiki/File:PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg
"Enlarge")

Bonneville crater and _Spirit_ rover's lander

The [dichotomy](/wiki/Martian_dichotomy "Martian dichotomy") of Martian
topography is striking: northern plains flattened by lava flows contrast with
the southern highlands, pitted and cratered by ancient impacts. Research in
2008 has presented evidence regarding a theory proposed in 1980 postulating
that, four billion years ago, the Northern Hemisphere of Mars was struck by an
object one-tenth to two-thirds the size of Earth's [Moon](/wiki/Moon "Moon").
If validated, this would make the Northern Hemisphere of Mars the site of an
[impact crater](/wiki/Impact_crater "Impact crater") 10,600 by 8,500
kilometres (6,600 by 5,300 mi) in size, or roughly the area of Europe, Asia,
and Australia combined, surpassing the [South Pole–Aitken
basin](/wiki/South_Pole%E2%80%93Aitken_basin "South Pole–Aitken basin") as the
largest impact crater in the Solar System.[19][20]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA18381-Mars-
FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg/220px-PIA18381-Mars-
FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-
After28March.jpg)](/wiki/File:PIA18381-Mars-
FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-
After28March.jpg "Enlarge")

Fresh [asteroid](/wiki/Asteroid "Asteroid") impact on Mars at [3°20′N
219°23′E﻿ / ﻿3.34°N 219.38°E﻿ / 3.34;
219.38](//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Mars&params=3.34_N_219.38_E_globe:Mars).
These before and after images of the same site were taken on the Martian
afternoons of 27 and 28 March 2012 respectively
([MRO](/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter "Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter")).[164]

Mars is scarred by a number of impact craters: a total of 43,000 craters with
a diameter of 5 kilometres (3.1 mi) or greater have been found.[165] The
largest confirmed of these is the [Hellas impact basin](/wiki/Hellas_Planitia
"Hellas Planitia"), a light [albedo feature](/wiki/Albedo_feature "Albedo
feature") clearly visible from Earth.[166] Due to the smaller mass and size of
Mars, the probability of an object colliding with the planet is about half
that of Earth. Mars is located closer to the [asteroid
belt](/wiki/Asteroid_belt "Asteroid belt"), so it has an increased chance of
being struck by materials from that source. Mars is more likely to be struck
by short-period [comets](/wiki/Comet "Comet"), _i.e._ , those that lie within
the orbit of [Jupiter](/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter").[167] In spite of this, there
are far fewer craters on Mars compared with the Moon, because the atmosphere
of Mars provides protection against small meteors and surface modifying
processes have erased some craters.

Martian craters can have a morphology that suggests the ground became wet
after the meteor impacted.[168]

#### Volcanoes

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg/220px-
Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg)](/wiki/File:Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg "Enlarge")

[Viking 1](/wiki/Viking_1 "Viking 1") image of [Olympus
Mons](/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons"). The volcano and related terrain are
approximately 550 km (340 mi) across.

Main article: [Volcanology of Mars](/wiki/Volcanology_of_Mars "Volcanology of
Mars")

The [shield volcano](/wiki/Shield_volcano "Shield volcano") [Olympus
Mons](/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons") ( _Mount Olympus_ ) is an extinct
volcano in the vast upland region [Tharsis](/wiki/Tharsis "Tharsis"), which
contains several other large volcanoes. Olympus Mons is roughly three times
the height of [Mount Everest](/wiki/Mount_Everest "Mount Everest"), which in
comparison stands at just over 8.8 kilometres (5.5 mi).[169] It is either the
tallest or second-tallest mountain in the Solar System, depending on how it is
measured, with various sources giving figures ranging from about 21 to 27
kilometres (13 to 17 mi) high.[170][171]

#### Tectonic sites

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg/220px-016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg)](/wiki/File:016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg "Enlarge")

[Valles Marineris](/wiki/Valles_Marineris "Valles Marineris") ( _[2001 Mars
Odyssey](/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey "2001 Mars Odyssey")_ )

The large canyon, [Valles Marineris](/wiki/Valles_Marineris "Valles
Marineris") (Latin for "[Mariner](/wiki/Mariner_program "Mariner program")
Valleys", also known as Agathadaemon in the old canal maps), has a length of
4,000 kilometres (2,500 mi) and a depth of up to 7 kilometres (4.3 mi). The
length of Valles Marineris is equivalent to the length of Europe and extends
across one-fifth the circumference of Mars. By comparison, the [Grand
Canyon](/wiki/Grand_Canyon "Grand Canyon") on Earth is only 446 kilometres
(277 mi) long and nearly 2 kilometres (1.2 mi) deep. Valles Marineris was
formed due to the swelling of the Tharsis area, which caused the crust in the
area of Valles Marineris to collapse. In 2012, it was proposed that Valles
Marineris is not just a [graben](/wiki/Graben "Graben"), but a plate boundary
where 150 kilometres (93 mi) of [transverse motion](/wiki/Transform_fault
"Transform fault") has occurred, making Mars a planet with possibly a
two-[tectonic plate](/wiki/Tectonic_plate "Tectonic plate")
arrangement.[172][173]

#### Holes

Images from the [Thermal Emission Imaging
System](/wiki/Thermal_Emission_Imaging_System "Thermal Emission Imaging
System") (THEMIS) aboard NASA's [Mars Odyssey orbiter](/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey
"2001 Mars Odyssey") have revealed seven possible [cave](/wiki/Cave "Cave")
entrances on the flanks of the volcano [Arsia Mons](/wiki/Arsia_Mons "Arsia
Mons").[174] The caves, named after loved ones of their discoverers, are
collectively known as the "seven sisters".[175] Cave entrances measure from
100 to 252 metres (328 to 827 ft) wide and they are estimated to be at least
73 to 96 metres (240 to 315 ft) deep. Because light does not reach the floor
of most of the caves, it is possible that they extend much deeper than these
lower estimates and widen below the surface. "Dena" is the only exception; its
floor is visible and was measured to be 130 metres (430 ft) deep. The
interiors of these caverns may be protected from micrometeoroids, UV
radiation, [solar flares](/wiki/Solar_flare "Solar flare") and high energy
particles that bombard the planet's surface.[176]

### Atmosphere

Main article: [Atmosphere of Mars](/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars "Atmosphere of
Mars")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mars_atmosphere.jpg/170px-
Mars_atmosphere.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mars_atmosphere.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:Mars_atmosphere.jpg "Enlarge")

The tenuous [atmosphere of Mars](/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars "Atmosphere of
Mars") visible on the horizon

Mars lost its [magnetosphere](/wiki/Magnetosphere "Magnetosphere") 4 billion
years ago,[177] possibly because of numerous asteroid strikes,[178] so the
[solar wind](/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind") interacts directly with the
Martian [ionosphere](/wiki/Ionosphere "Ionosphere"), lowering the atmospheric
density by stripping away atoms from the outer layer. Both _[Mars Global
Surveyor](/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor "Mars Global Surveyor")_ and _[Mars
Express](/wiki/Mars_Express "Mars Express")_ have detected ionised atmospheric
particles trailing off into space behind Mars,[177][179] and this atmospheric
loss is being studied by the [MAVEN](/wiki/MAVEN "MAVEN") orbiter. Compared to
Earth, the [atmosphere](/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere "Celestial body
atmosphere") of Mars is quite rarefied. [Atmospheric
pressure](/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure "Atmospheric pressure") on the surface
today ranges from a low of 30 [Pa](/wiki/Pascal_\(unit\) "Pascal \(unit\)")
(0.0044 [psi](/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch "Pounds per square inch")) on
[Olympus Mons](/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons") to over 1,155 Pa (0.1675
psi) in [Hellas Planitia](/wiki/Hellas_Planitia "Hellas Planitia"), with a
mean pressure at the surface level of 600 Pa (0.087 psi).[180] The highest
atmospheric density on Mars is equal to that found 35 kilometres (22 mi)[181]
above Earth's surface. The resulting mean surface pressure is only 0.6% of
that of Earth 101.3 kPa (14.69 psi). The [scale height](/wiki/Scale_height
"Scale height") of the atmosphere is about 10.8 kilometres (6.7 mi),[182]
which is higher than Earth's, 6 kilometres (3.7 mi), because the surface
[gravity of Mars](/wiki/Gravity_of_Mars "Gravity of Mars") is only about 38%
of Earth's, an effect offset by both the lower temperature and 50% higher
average molecular weight of the atmosphere of Mars.

The atmosphere of Mars consists of about 96% [carbon
dioxide](/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide"), 1.93% [argon](/wiki/Argon
"Argon") and 1.89% [nitrogen](/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen") along with traces of
[oxygen](/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") and water.[10][183] The atmosphere is quite
dusty, containing particulates about 1.5 [µm](/wiki/%CE%9Cm "Μm") in diameter
which give the Martian sky a [tawny](/wiki/Tawny_\(color\) "Tawny \(color\)")
color when seen from the surface.[184] It may take on a
[pink](/wiki/Pink_\(color\) "Pink \(color\)") hue due to [iron
oxide](/wiki/Iron_oxide "Iron oxide") particles suspended in it.[17]

#### Methane

Main article: [Methane on Mars](/wiki/Methane_on_Mars "Methane on Mars")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-
MethaneSource-20141216.png/220px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-
MethaneSource-20141216.png)](/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-
MethaneSource-20141216.png)

[](/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png
"Enlarge")

Potential sources and sinks of [methane](/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane
"Atmosphere of Mars") (CH  
4) on Mars

[Methane](/wiki/Methane "Methane") has been detected in the [Martian
atmosphere](/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane "Atmosphere of Mars");[185][186]
it occurs in extended plumes, and the profiles imply that the methane is
released from discrete regions. The concentration of methane fluctuates from
about 0.24 [ppb](/wiki/Parts_per_billion "Parts per billion") during the
northern winter to about 0.65 [ppb](/wiki/Parts_per_billion "Parts per
billion") during the summer.[187]

Estimates of its lifetime range from 0.6 to 4 years,[188][189] so its presence
indicates that an active source of the gas must be present. Methane could be
produced by non-biological process such as
[serpentinization](/wiki/Serpentinite "Serpentinite") involving water, carbon
dioxide, and the mineral [olivine](/wiki/Olivine "Olivine"), which is known to
be common on Mars.[190] [Methanogenic](/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen")
[microbial](/wiki/Microorganism "Microorganism") life forms in the subsurface
are among possible sources. But even if rover missions determine that
microscopic Martian life is the source of the methane, the life forms likely
reside far below the surface, outside of the rover's reach.[191]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-
Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg/300px-PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-
Views-20141014.jpg)](/wiki/File:PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-
Views-20141014.jpg)

[](/wiki/File:PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg "Enlarge")

[Escaping atmosphere](/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars "Atmosphere of Mars") on Mars
([carbon](/wiki/Carbon "Carbon"), [oxygen](/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), and
[hydrogen](/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen")) by [MAVEN](/wiki/MAVEN "MAVEN") in
[UV](/wiki/Ultraviolet%E2%80%93visible_spectroscopy "Ultraviolet–visible
spectroscopy")[192]

#### Aurora

In 1994, the European Space Agency's Mars Express found an ultraviolet glow
coming from "magnetic umbrellas" in the Southern Hemisphere. Mars does not
have a global magnetic field which guides charged particles entering the
atmosphere. Mars has multiple umbrella-shaped magnetic fields mainly in the
Southern Hemisphere, which are remnants of a global field that decayed
billions of years ago.

In late December 2014, NASA's MAVEN spacecraft detected evidence of widespread
auroras in Mars's Northern Hemisphere and descended to approximately 20–30°
North latitude of Mars's equator. The particles causing the aurora penetrated
into the Martian atmosphere, creating auroras below 100 km above the surface,
Earth's auroras range from 100 km to 500 km above the surface. Magnetic fields
in the solar wind drape over Mars, into the atmosphere, and the charged
particles follow the solar wind magnetic field lines into the atmosphere,
causing auroras to occur outside the magnetic umbrellas.[193]

On 18 March 2015, NASA reported the detection of an [aurora](/wiki/Aurora
"Aurora") that is not fully understood and an unexplained dust cloud in the
[atmosphere of Mars](/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars "Atmosphere of Mars").[194]

In September 2017, NASA reported [radiation](/wiki/Radiation "Radiation")
levels on the surface of the planet Mars were temporarily
[doubled](/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_\(radiation\) "Orders of magnitude
\(radiation\)"), and were associated with an [aurora](/wiki/Aurora "Aurora")
25 times brighter than any observed earlier, due to a massive, and unexpected,
[solar storm](/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection "Coronal mass ejection") in the
middle of the month.[195]

### Climate

Main article: [Climate of Mars](/wiki/Climate_of_Mars "Climate of Mars")

Of all the planets in the Solar System, the seasons of Mars are the most
Earth-like, due to the similar tilts of the two planets' rotational axes. The
lengths of the Martian seasons are about twice those of Earth's because Mars's
greater distance from the Sun leads to the Martian year being about two Earth
years long. Martian surface temperatures vary from lows of about −143 °C (−225
°F) at the winter polar caps[13] to highs of up to 35 °C (95 °F) in equatorial
summer.[14] The wide range in temperatures is due to the thin atmosphere which
cannot store much solar heat, the low atmospheric pressure, and the low
[thermal inertia](/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity "Volumetric heat capacity")
of Martian soil.[196] The planet is 1.52 times as far from the Sun as Earth,
resulting in just 43% of the amount of sunlight.[197]

If Mars had an Earth-like orbit, its seasons would be similar to Earth's
because its [axial tilt](/wiki/Axial_tilt "Axial tilt") is similar to Earth's.
The comparatively large [eccentricity](/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity "Orbital
eccentricity") of the Martian orbit has a significant effect. Mars is near
[perihelion](/wiki/Apsis "Apsis") when it is summer in the Southern Hemisphere
and winter in the north, and near [aphelion](/wiki/Apsis "Apsis") when it is
winter in the Southern Hemisphere and summer in the north. As a result, the
seasons in the Southern Hemisphere are more extreme and the seasons in the
northern are milder than would otherwise be the case. The summer temperatures
in the south can be warmer than the equivalent summer temperatures in the
north by up to 30 °C (54 °F).[198]

Mars has the largest [dust storms](/wiki/Dust_storm "Dust storm") in the Solar
System, reaching speeds of over 160 km/h (100 mph). These can vary from a
storm over a small area, to gigantic storms that cover the entire planet. They
tend to occur when Mars is closest to the Sun, and have been shown to increase
the global temperature.[199]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA22487-Mars-
